1	"""PyTorch reference for a Kimi-Linear W4A16 hybrid decode unit (batch=1).
2	
3	Correctness oracle, NOT the fast baseline (baseline.py) or the SOTA ceiling.
4	One repeating motif of Kimi-Linear-48B-A3B -- 3 KDA (gated-delta linear attn)
5	layers + 1 MLA (multi-head latent attention) layer, each + a 64-expert MoE FFN
6	-- decoded one token at a time, with the big projection weights stored as
7	**W4A16** (int4 weights, group-128 asymmetric, bf16 accumulation; the AWQ/GPTQ
8	format that open-source inference actually ships).
9	
10	Why W4A16 and not fp8: at batch-1 decode the bottleneck is *weight memory
11	traffic*. int4 weights are 0.25x the bytes of bf16 (4x less traffic), and a
12	fused dequant-GEMV realizes that win in the memory-bound regime where decode
… +360 more lines

[Read] /home/shadeform/kernelbench.com/benchmarks/mega/outputs/runs/20260715_204551_kinetic-claude_kinetic-0715_02_kimi_linear_decode/repo/problems/02_kimi_linear_decode/baseline.py
tool
1	"""Optimized-PyTorch W4A16 baseline for the Kimi-Linear decode unit.
2	
3	The floor the solution must beat -- NOT the oracle (reference.py). Same int4
4	weights, same math, written the competent pure-PyTorch way: bf16 accumulation
5	and batched MoE expert dequant instead of the reference's fp32 per-expert loop.
6	
7	It still *materializes* each dequantized bf16 weight before the matmul (int4
8	read + bf16 write + bf16 read = ~9x the traffic of a fused path). That is the
9	whole point left on the table: stock PyTorch cannot fuse the int4 unpack into
10	the GEMV, so beating this baseline requires a custom fused dequant-GEMV kernel.
11	`score = baseline_latency / solution_latency`.
12	"""

tool
1	"""Correctness runner for the Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit.
2	
3	Per-step correctness, not a chained trajectory: for each seed and a couple of
4	context lengths, the reference and the solution are given an IDENTICAL fed
5	state and one new token, each runs ONE decode step, and we compare the
6	next-token hidden plus the updated decode state (KDA recurrent state, MLA latent
7	cache) by cosine similarity. Cosine sim is robust to bf16/fp8 precision but
8	still rejects wrong answers. Chaining steps would just measure how fast two
9	precisions diverge, which is not a kernel-correctness signal.
10	"""
11	from __future__ import annotations

reasoning
Baseline: ~7.6-9.2 ms/tok. Total int4 weight buffers = 1068 MB. At 1.8 TB/s theoretical, streaming all weights once = 1068e6/1.8e12undefined ≈ 593 µs. Realistically ~700-800 µs. So the roofline is roughly 10x faster than baseline. The dominant cost: weight streaming (all weights per token — every QuantLinear in 4 attention layers + 8 active experts + shared expert × 4 MoE blocks). Let me compute the weight bytes more carefully: Config: hidden d=2304, kda_heads=32, head_dim=128 → C=4096. Per KDA layer: - q,k,v,g proj: 4 × (2304×4096) params → int4 bytes: 4 × 2304×4096×0.5 = 18.87 MB (0.5 byte/param) plus scales/zeros: 4 × 18 groups × 4096 × 2 dtypes... scales (2304/128=18, 4096) bf16 = 18×4096×2 = 147KB; zeros same → 295KB per linearundefined ≈ 1.6% overhead. - o_proj: (4096, 2304) → 4096×2304×0.5 = 4.72 MB. - So KDA linears = 5 × 2304×4096×0.5 = 23.6 MB + ~1.5MB sq. Per MLA layer: - q_proj: (2304, 32×192=6144) → 2304×6144/2 = 7.08 MB - kv_a: (2304, 576) → 0.66 MB - kv_b: (512, 32×256=8192) → 2.1 MB - o_proj: (4096, 2304) → 4.72 MB Total MLAundefined ≈ 14.6 MB Per MoE (64 experts + 1 shared): - gate/up: (2304, 1024) each → 2304×1024/2 = 1.18 MB per expert per weight - down: (1024, 2304) → same 1.18 MB - per expert 3.54 MB; active 8 routed + 1 shared = 9 × 3.54 = 31.9 MB per MoE block. Per block: KDA 23.6 + MoE 31.9 = 55.5 MB (K blocks ×3), MLA block 14.6+31.9 = 46.5 MB. Total per token streamed = 3×55.5 + 46.5 = 213 MB... But wait — the model has ALL experts' weights (1068 MB), but per token we only stream active experts (8 of 64 + 1 shared). So the actual per-token stream = 4 blocks × (attn weights) + 4 × MoE(active). Let me recompute: KDA block: 5 linears × 2304×4096×0.5 B = 23.6 MB (including sq overhead ~6.3% → check: scales/zeros per linear: (in/128)×out×2×2 bytes. For (2304,4096): 18×4096×4 = 295KB = 0.295MB vs 4.72MB w_q = 6.25%. Hmm that's substantial. Total per linear 5.01MB; ×5 = 25MB.) Attention totals: - KDA ×3: 3 × 25.0 = 75.1 MB - MLA: q_proj 7.08 + 0.46 sq = 7.54; kv_a 2304×576/2=0.663 + sq(18×576×4=41KB) = 0.70; kv_b 512×8192/2=2.1 + sq(4×8192×4=131KB)=2.23; o_proj 4.72+0.295=5.01. Total 15.5 MB. MoE per block: active experts 9 (8+1) × 3 weights × (2304×1024×0.5 + 18×1024×4) = 9 × 3 × (1.18 + 0.074) = 9 × 3.76 = 33.9 MB. ×4 blocks = 135.4 MB. Total per tokenundefined ≈ 75.1 + 15.5 + 135.4 = 226 MB. At 1.8 TB/s → 125 µs. At realistic ~1.5 TB/s → 150 µs. Hmm wait, that assumed we stream everything per token. MLA cache at 16384: kv_b dequant... The MLA attention over 16k context: kv_b applied to c_kv is (L, 512) @ (512, 8192)?? That's the kvb materialization — for decode, computing k_nope and v for ALL past tokens every step costs L×512×0.5×2 bytes = 16K×512 = 8.4MB (weight re-read? kv_b is re-streamed anyway) plus compute L×512×8192 FLOPs = 16k×512×8192×2 = 137 GFLOP — that's way too much! 137 GFLOP at bf16 ~ 100+ TFLOPs/s... that's over a millisecond. Hmm wait, einsum: for each of L=16384 tokens: (512,) @ (512, 8192) → that's L times a GEMV: 16384 × 512×8192 MACs = 68.7 GMAC = 137 GFLOP. At bf16 tensorcore on RTX PRO 6000 (~200 TFLOPS bf16 dense?) that's 0.7ms. That's HUGE relative to 125 µs of weight streaming. Actually more importantly: kv_b weight (2.1 MB int4) must be read once per token anyway. The issue is compute: 137 GFLOP per decode step for the kvb materialization over the whole cache. That's the "absorb" question named in the prompt: "the MLA absorb-vs-materialize fork in MLA decides the whole thing". Alternative: absorb. Standard MLA decode trick: attention scores = q_nope^T k_nope where k_nope = c_kv @ W_kvb_nope part → q^T W_UK c: precompute q' = q_nope @ W_{kv_b nope part} (a (H,128)@(H·128 split of kv_b)... Let me think. k_nope[l, h, d_n] = sum_c c_kv[l, c] * Wkv_b_nope[c, h, d_n] scores[l, h] = sum_d q_nope[h, d] * k_nope[l, h, d] = sum_c (sum_d q_nope[h,d] W[c, h, d]) * c_kv[l, c] = sum_c q_abs[h, c] c_kv[l, c] where q_abs[h, c] = sum_d q_nope[h, d] * W_nope[c, h*128+d]. Computing q_abs: H × 128 × 512 MACs = 32×128×512 = 2.1 GMAC = 4.2 MFLOP... trivial. Then scores = q_abs @ c_kv^T: H × L × 512 MACs = 32 × 16384 × 512 = 268M MACs = 537 MFLOP... That's still sizeable but 256× less than 137 GFLOP. Similarly output: o[h, dv] = sum_l p[l,h] v[l,h,dv], v[l,h,:] = c_kv[l,:] @ W_vpart[:, h, :]. o[h,dv] = sum_c (sum_l p[l,h] c_kv[l,c]) W_v[c, h, dv] = (p·c_kv)[h,c] then @ W_v part: H×512 MACs... compute per head: first ctx vector u[h,c] = sum_l p[l,h] c_kv[l,c] (H×L×512 = 537 MFLOP), then o = u @ W_v reorganization: H×512×128 = 4.2 MFLOP. Total absorbed MLAundefined ≈ 1.1 GFLOP vs 137 GFLOP materialized. Massive. But scores computed this way aren't bit-identical: scores = q_nope · k_nope where k_nope = c_kv @ W in bf16 (reference computes kvb in fp32 after dequant? reference baseline: `kvb = self.kv_b(st["c_kv"]).view(...).float()` — QuantLinear forward does fp32 matmul of dequantized weight. Order of accumulation differs. Cosine sim tolerance 0.98 gives room. q_abs = q_nope @ W means scores = sum over c of (sum over d of q[d] W[c,d]) c_kv[c] — mathematically identical, just reassociated. fp32 accumulation, small rounding differences. Fine for cosine 0.98. However, absorbing requires per-step dequant of the whole kv_b weight: W_nope part (512, H×128) and W_v part (512, H×128) — that's exactly the kv_b weight read: 2.1 MB int4, we need to read it anyway. But we need the dequantized values to both compute q_abs AND apply to u. That's fine: it's small compute (2×4.2 MFLOP), we can fuse dequant into those small GEMMs. So the absorbed MLA per-step cost: stream c_kv cache L×512 bf16 = 16.8 MB at 16K context (k_rope L×64 bf16 = 2.1 MB). Attention computeundefined ≈ 1.1 GFLOP. That's the real long-context cost: cache streaming ~19 MB vs weights 226 MB. OK. Alternatively materialize: costs L×512×0.5×... no wait, materialize costs reading c_kv once for kvb GEMM (L×512 bf16 = 16.8MB read), plus kv_b weight 2.1MB, then attention reads k_nope/v: L×H×128×2×2 = 16.8 MB × [...]. Total memory similar (~35-50 MB) but compute 137 GFLOP vs 1.1 GFLOP. Absorbed wins hugely. At 1.1 GFLOP and bf16... this is tensor-core-worthy GEMM-like compute: scores: q_abs (32, 512) × c_kv^T (512, 16384) → GEMM M=32, K=512, N=16384. In a Triton kernel, tl.dot works great. We'll need to handle it inside the megakernel. RoPE handling: scores also include q_rope · k_rope terms: (32×64) @ (64, 16384). Can fold: scores = [q_abs q_rope] @ [c_kv; k_rope]^T → keys dimension 512+64=576. Nice: concatenate conceptually. On the GPU we must do: 1. rmsnorm(x) + q/k/v/g GEMV + conv + gate/beta (KDA), S update, o GEMV, residual 2. MLA GEMVs, rope, cache append, attention GEMMs, o GEMV, residual 3. MoE: rmsnorm, router GEMV (bf16 2304×64), topk, 9 experts × 3 GEMVs each + silu, weighted sum, residual Dependencies between phases within the same kernel require grid-wide sync. Options: A. Single thread block (gridDim=1) with many warps, iterating. No grid sync needed (just block-level barriers). But one SM's worth of compute... a single SM can only use ~1/188 of memory bandwidth (RTX PRO 6000 has, let me estimate — SM120 Blackwell likely 188 SMs? Let me check device props). Way too slow: bandwidth per SM ~ 1.8TB/s / N_sm. With 188 SMs → ~10 GB/s per SM → 226 MB would take 22ms. Too slow. B. Persistent kernel with cooperative-groups grid sync (cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel). torch load_inline can build it. Grid sync between ~20-40 phases. Max occupancy: we need all blocks resident. With say 188 SMs × (1-4 blocks/SM)... it works; this is what "megakernel" decode implementations do (e.g., FlashInfer-style... actually like "gpt-fast" fused kernels, ParallelFormers' megakernel, etc.). Grid sync cost ~ 2-5 µs each on large grids... × 30 syncs = 60-150 µs overhead. Hmm, that's real but acceptable? Actually grid.sync() latency is typically ~1-2 µs at small grids (few hundred blocks). 30×2 = 60µs vs 125µs ideal compute — that halves efficiency. Reduce number of syncs by fusing more work between them. C. Triton approach: Triton has no grid sync. But we can do a spin-lock/atomic counter-based software barrier between phases — standard technique: each block arrives at barrier (atomicAdd), one block... all blocks spin on a counter until count == nBlocks. Deadlock-free iff all blocks are resident simultaneously → persistent grid sized ≤ max co-resident. This is what many Triton "megakernels" do (e.g., fused QKV kernels, the "Triton persistent matmul + barrier" pattern). It's legal: single kernel launch. Spin barriers in Triton need `tl.atomic_add` and a while loop with memory fences. Doable but tricky (need volatile loads... Triton `tl.load(ptr, volatile=True)` exists? There's `tl.atomic_add` with sem, and `tl.load(..., cache_modifier='.cv')` or volatile param exists). D. Split into sequential phases via multiple grids in ONE launch — not possible; a launch has one grid. E. CUDA C++ with cooperative groups via load_inline: grid.sync(). Clean. Requires -rdc? grid.sync() needs cooperative launch: cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel. load_inline with functions — we can launch via cudaLaunchCooperativeKernel in the C++ wrapper. RDC not needed for grid.sync (only for multi-device). F. Avoid global sync entirely: express the whole network as dependency-respecting tasks with atomics spin-waits on flags ("software pipelined megakernel"). Still, within a layer: q/k/v/g proj are independent → can be computed by different block slices concurrently without sync, then a flag-sync for conv/recurrence. This is essentially dataflow execution with fine-grained flags — like the "megakernel" from HazyResearch (ThunderKittens) / "mirage persistent kernel". This maximizes overlap (e.g., overlap attention cache read with MoE weight streaming? deps forbid). Now the GEMV kernel design (the bandwidth-critical core): y[0:N] = x[0:K] @ W_int4(K,N). Split N across blocks: each block handles [n0, n0+BN). Loop over K: read x[k-chunk] (fp32, from L2, redundant reads fine: x is 2304 fp32 = 9KB), read packed w bytes: w_q[k/2, n] — for k-loop with BN wide n: packed row (K/2, N): for chunk of k = 128 (one group), packed rows = 64 bytes rows × BN columns... Coalescing: threads in block read consecutive n for fixed packed-k row: row segment (64 consecutive packed rows?) — each read: w_q[kp, n0+tid] uint8 → 1 byte per thread → memory transaction 32 B per warp — poor (should be ≥ 128B). Better: reinterpret loads as uint32/uint4: each thread loads 16 bytes = 32 nibbles = 32 k-values for one n → thread handles one n? Then block BN = nthreads e.g. 128 threads × 16B = 2KB per row-load. Layout: row kp has N bytes; thread t handles n = n0+t: reads 16 consecutive packed rows (kp0..kp0+15) each 1 byte at column n — STRIDED (stride N). Terrible. Transpose approach: pre-transpose weights at load time to (N, K/2) layout: each output n → its packed weights contiguous (K/2 bytes + scales/zeros (K/128 each)). Then thread t handles output n_t: reads K/2 = 1152 bytes contiguous (2304-in) → vectorized readonly loads, fully coalesced IF consecutive threads read... thread0 reads n0's 1152 bytes, thread1 reads n1's... each thread streams a DIFFERENT contiguous region (column of original) — that's fine: total per iteration, warp reads 32 regions; uncoalesced across threads (stride 1152B) but each thread's own stream is contiguous → memory system sees 32 concurrent sequential streams per warp; DRAM efficiency comes from 128B line utilization: warp reads 32 different lines per load instruction → 1 line per thread... each line used fully eventually (thread continues reading it). With 16B vector loads: LDG.128 per thread → warp = 32×16B from 32 different 128B lines = 32 transactions per instruction = full efficiency IF each line fully consumed (yes, sequentially). Actually uncoalesced 16B loads: each request = 128B sector fetch? Modern GPUs fetch 32B sectors; 16B load wastes half sector unless subsequent load hits same sector — it does (next 16B in line comes in the next iteration; L2 caches it). This "each thread owns one output column, streams contiguous bytes" is the classic llama.cpp-style GEMV layout and it achieves ~85-95% of DRAM peak. Alternative "block-N, thread-K" split: warp cooperatively loads CONTIGUOUS packed row chunk (coalesced 128B+), then each thread holds bytes for 32 k's × its n's... then FMA x[k]·w[k,n] needs cross-thread reduction over... each thread's w covers different k's for same n? For y[n] = Σ_k x[k] w[k,n]: coalesced loads along n (row-major (K/2,N)): warp loads bytes for (kp, n0..n0+127) → each thread 4 bytes = 8 k's × 1 n? Distributed: each thread accumulates y[n_local] over the k's it loaded (8 per 4B load), then K-loop covers all kp → each thread ends with FULL sum for its n? NO — thread covers (all kp, its n): each load gives 8 consecutive k (4 bytes × 2 nibbles), k-loop iterates kp by +8*warpsize?? Standard Triton GEMV: tl.load of a (BK, BN) tile of packed bytes (2D block load is coalesced along n), unpack to (2*BK) k's, multiply with x tile (broadcast), tl.sum over k axis → y[BN]. Triton handles the vectorization automatically (tl.load with contiguous last-dim). That's the easy path and gets good bandwidth. Barrier implementation (standard): ``` barrier_id += 1 tl.debug_barrier() # block-level sync so all threads... hmm all threads in block run same code if tl.program_id... ``` Spin barrier across blocks: each block does: old = tl.atomic_add(counter, 1, sem='acq_rel', scope='gpu') executed by ONE thread (or all threads? use one designated... in Triton everything is block-level SIMT across tensors; atomic_add on scalar pointer adds once per... tl.atomic_add with scalar does ONE atomic op per program (scalar semantics). So: `tl.atomic_add(cnt, 1, sem='release', scope='gpu')` then `while tl.atomic_add(cnt, 0, sem='acquire', scope='gpu') < target: pass`. But all programs must arrive. Target = nblocks * barrier_seq (counter monotonic across barriers; reset each barrier: use per-phase target = phase*nblocks). Also need to ensure WRITES before barrier visible: release semantics on the add ensures prior writes visible to acquire readers. Plus L2 is coherent. This is the classic pattern and does work in Triton (used by several projects — e.g. "persistent kernel barrier in triton" in various blog posts; risks: compiler hoisting loads out of spin loop — volatile/atomic avoids). +"""Isolate: pure u8 load+sum vs dequant math vs reduce shapes."""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl, time
+
+@triton.jit
+def pure_load(wq_ptr, y_ptr, N, KT, BN: tl.constexpr, BKP: tl.constexpr):
+    pid = tl.program_id(0)
+    nn = tl.cdiv(N // 2, BN)
+    pn = pid % nn
+    pk = pid // nn
+    offs_n = pn * BN + tl.arange(0, BN)
+    acc = tl.zeros((BN,), dtype=tl.int32)
+    for it in range(KT):
+        kp = pk * KT * BKP + it * BKP + tl.arange(0, BKP)

+"""v3: correct full-GPU grids, cold-DRAM emulation via buffer ring, 3 variants."""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl, time
+
+DTYPE = torch.bfloat16
+
+def quantize_ref(w, group=128):
+    K, N = w.shape
+    ng = K // group
+    wg = w.view(ng, group, N).float()
+    wmin = wg.min(dim=1, keepdim=True).values
+    wmax = wg.max(dim=1, keepdim=True).values
+    scales = (wmax - wmin).clamp_min(1e-8) / 15.0
+    zeros = (-wmin / scales).round().clamp(0, 15)

+"""Find real DRAM read ceiling: grid-stride u8 reader with various configs."""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl, time
+
+@triton.jit
+def reader(xs, out, n_iters, PER_PROG_BYTES, BS: tl.constexpr, WARM: tl.constexpr):
+    pid = tl.program_id(0)
+    nprog = tl.num_programs(0)
+    base = pid.to(tl.int64) * PER_PROG_BYTES
+    acc = tl.zeros((BS,), dtype=tl.int32)
+    for i in range(n_iters):
+        u = tl.load(xs + base + i * BS + tl.arange(0, BS))
+        acc += u.to(tl.int32)
+    tl.store(out + tl.arange(0, BS) + pid * BS, acc.to(tl.float32))
… +19 more lines

So the GEMV kernels are only 3x off. Note key difference: the reader has each program reading CONTIGUOUS bytes, BS=4096; GEMV loads 2D tiles of 64-128 contiguous bytes per row. The DRAM/L2 sector behavior with row-width 64B: 32 threads read 64B... 128B L2 lines half-used? Wait — tiles: A variant: (BKP=64, BN=128): row = 128 contiguous bytes of n. That's a full 128B line per row. Rows within tile are strided 4096B apart. 64 rows → 64 separate lines per load-instr group. Should be FINE for DRAM (full lines used). Yet 0.3 TB/s. THIS is why megakernel design matters: barriers stall the memory pipe. Barrier count × ~1-2µs memory-ramp directly adds. With ~20 barriers → 20-40µs of ramp overhead. Matches earlier estimate. Prefetching weights into L2 doesn't depend on activations. Implement: after arriving at barrier, blocks issue prefetch loads (touch addresses) for next stage's weights, then spin. DRAM works during barrier instead of idling. Even better: SOFTWARE PIPELINE via flags: since stage N+1's GEMV needs stage N's output only for the x-vector, and x-vector computation is the LAST thing gated... +"""v4 tuned: 1-iter-per-GEMV timing ring, eviction policies, stages sweep."""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl, time
+
+DTYPE = torch.bfloat16
+
+def quantize_ref(w, group=128):
+    K, N = w.shape
+    wg = w.view(K//group, group, N).float()
+    wmin = wg.min(dim=1, keepdim=True).values; wmax = wg.max(dim=1, keepdim=True).values
+    scales = (wmax - wmin).clamp_min(1e-8) / 15.0
+    zeros = (-wmin / scales).round().clamp(0, 15)
+    w_q = ((wg / scales) + zeros).round().clamp(0, 15).to(torch.uint8).view(K, N)
+    lo = w_q[0::2] & 0xF; hi = w_q[1::2] & 0xF

+"""Decisive probes: geometry-only vs math, for GEMV-shaped access."""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl, time
+
+def evtime(call, iters=30):
+    for _ in range(3): call(0)
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    evs = [(torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True), torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)) for _ in range(iters)]
+    for i in range(iters):
+        evs[i][0].record(); call(i); evs[i][1].record()
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    return sorted(a.elapsed_time(b) for a, b in evs)[iters // 4] * 1e-3
+
+K, N = 2304, 4096

+"""Validate: manual prefetch pipelines the GEMV. Row-scan (KP,N) layout, per-thread acc."""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl, time
+
+def evtime(call, iters=30):
+    for _ in range(3): call(0)
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    evs = [(torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True), torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)) for _ in range(iters)]
+    for i in range(iters):
+        evs[i][0].record(); call(i); evs[i][1].record()
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    return sorted(a.elapsed_time(b) for a, b in evs)[iters // 4] * 1e-3
+

+        evs[i][0].record(); call(i); evs[i][1].record()

+    ts = sorted(a.elapsed_time(b) for a, b in evs)
+    return ts[iters // 4] * 1e-3, ts[iters // 2] * 1e-3

+++ b//ephemeral/mega-outputs/runs/20260715_204551_kinetic-claude_kinetic-0715_02_kimi_linear_decode/repo/problems/02_kimi_linear_decode/scratch/probe_ss.py

+"""Steady-state GEMV: kernel loops over 8 slices internally to amortize launch/ramp."""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl, time
+
+def evtime(call, iters=20):
+    for _ in range(3): call()
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    evs = [(torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True), torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)) for _ in range(iters)]
+    for i in range(iters):
+        evs[i][0].record(); call(); evs[i][1].record()
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    ts = sorted(a.elapsed_time(b) for a, b in evs)
+    return ts[iters // 4] * 1e-3
+

MOST of the time producers stay ahead → no stalling. +"""GEMV contenders R2: small f32 tiles vs int8-MMA. Steady-state via 8-slice loop."""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl, time
+
+def evtime(call, iters=20):
+    for _ in range(3): call()
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    evs = [(torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True), torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)) for _ in range(iters)]
+    for i in range(iters):
+        evs[i][0].record(); call(); evs[i][1].record()
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    ts = sorted(a.elapsed_time(b) for a, b in evs)
+    return ts[iters // 4] * 1e-3
+

Actually Triton scalar ops are "uniform" — generated once, executed by... hmm — in Triton, a scalar tl.atomic_add compiles to an atomic executed by ALL THREADS?? or one? Triton semantics: scalar tensor operations are executed redundantly on all threads. An atomic_add on scalar would execute PER THREAD → NTHREAD increments!!! WRONG. Workaround: make it a 1-element TENSOR atomic: tl.atomic_add(cnt_ptr + tl.arange(0,1), 1) — a (1,)-shaped tensor atomic: one element → but which thread? Triton (1,)-tensor op executes on ONE thread (the one owning element 0)undefined ✓ pattern used in persistent-kernel examples: `tl.atomic_add(ptr + tl.arange(0, 1), 1)`.undefined ✓ And spin read: `tl.atomic_add(ptr + tl.arange(0,1), 0, sem="acquire")` → (1,) → broadcast... comparison → tl.reduce... spin: `while tl.sum(tl.atomic_add(ptr+tl.arange(0,1), 0)) < target: pass`. Sum reduces (1,) to scalar without extra ops. OK. BUT does the while condition re-evaluate atomics per iteration? +"""megakernel primitives: spin barrier cost, libdevice, atomic publication."""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl, time
+
+def evtime(call, iters=50):
+    for _ in range(5): call()
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    evs = [(torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True), torch.cuda.Event(enable_timing=True)) for _ in range(iters)]
+    for i in range(iters):
+        evs[i][0].record(); call(); evs[i][1].record()
+    torch.cuda.synchronize()
+    ts = sorted(a.elapsed_time(b) for a, b in evs)
+    return ts[iters // 4] * 1e-3
+

+"""W4A16 Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit: single-launch persistent megakernel.
+
+Everything (all 4 attention layers, all int4 dequant-GEMVs, KDA recurrence,
+MLA latent attention, MoE router+experts, RMSNorms, residuals, state updates)
+runs inside ONE @triton.jit kernel launch per step() call.
+
+Strategy:
+  - Persistent grid (188*2 blocks, spin grid-wide barriers between stages).
+  - int4 GEMV via int8-MMA: activations are quantized to int8 per 128-group
+    on the fly (amax), dot() in int8, per-group dequant correction in fp32:
+    y_g = s_w*xs*(sum_i xq*u)/16 - s_w*z_w*(sum xq)*xs  (x replicated 16x for
+    the tensor-core M dim; summed and rescaled by 1/16).
+  - MLA attention uses the "absorbed" form: q_abs = q_nope @ W_nope, so both

+"""W4A16 Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit: single-launch persistent megakernel.
+
+The entire per-token forward - 3 KDA layers + 1 MLA layer, each with a
+64-expert MoE FFN, RMSNorms, residuals, KDA conv+recurrence state update and

+    (x replicated 16x on the tensor-core M axis, summed, rescaled by 1/16).

+"""W4A16 Kimi-Linear hybrid decode unit: single-launch Triton megakernel.
+
+The entire per-token forward - 3 KDA layers + 1 MLA layer, each with a
+64-expert MoE FFN, RMSNorms, residuals, KDA conv+recurrence state update and
+MLA latent-cache attention - runs inside ONE @triton.jit kernel launch.
+
+Design:
+  - Persistent grid (NBLK blocks) with spin grid-wide barriers between
+    statically-scheduled stages (task loops strided over blocks).
+  - Every int4 GEMV is a fused dequant-GEMV: activations are quantized to
+    int8 per 128-group on the fly (amax), the x*int4 dot runs on int8 tensor
+    cores, with an exact per-group dequant correction in fp32:
+        y_g[n] = s[n]*xs*sum_k xq_k*u_kn - s[n]*z[n]*xs*sum_k xq_k

CONCLUSION: int8-x quantization per-128 group has inherent ~low-SNR-per-element on small elements. bf16 reference also rounds per-element but to ITS OWN 8 mantissa bits of that element — small values keep full RELATIVE precision. int8 group quantization TRADES per-element relative precision for... this is the fundamental weakness. It causes ~5-10e-2 relative noise in canceled sums and could break the 0.98 final target — currently we see its effect: q_act 0.978, and downstream more. Mitigation options: (a) fp16/bf16 path for x (not int8): f32 A8 at 0.95 TB/s (vs 1.27) — 25% slower but ~exact (bf16-ish SNR). Given cos risk, maybe take it. (b) int8 per-32 subgroup quantization? Actually simpler: x_f32 → xq_hi = int8_rn(x/xs), resid = x − xs·xhi; xq_lo = int8_rn(resid/(xs/128)): x = xs·xhi + (xs/128)·xlo + ε (ε ~ xs/256): MUCH better SNR (~2^-14 per element relative to group scale... per element absolute error ~ xs·2^-8... for small values in a group with big amax: still absolute floor). +"""Does data written before a spin-barrier arrive become visible after it?"""
+import torch, triton, triton.language as tl
+
+@triton.jit
+def _bar(CNT, target):
+    tl.debug_barrier()
+    tl.atomic_add(CNT + tl.arange(0, 1), 1, sem="release")
+    while tl.sum(tl.atomic_add(CNT + tl.arange(0, 1), 0, sem="acquire")) < target:
+        pass

+    tl.debug_barrier()
